10.1016/j.jmp.2013.07.002
When it comes to the measurement of recognition memory, there are two distinctly different theoretical frameworks; one framework is exemplified by strength-based recognition memory models, and the other framework is exemplified by multinomial processing tree (MPT) models. See  Malmberg (2008)  and  Yonelinas (2002)  for extensive reviews of recognition memory from the perspective of strength-based models, and see  Batchelder and Riefer (1999)  and  Erdfelder et al. (2009)  for general reviews of the MPT literature that include models of recognition memory. Strength models and MPT models have different goals and are based on different theoretical characterizations of event memory. Although the strength-based models are designed specifically for recognition memory tests, a subset of the MPT models have been designed for recognition memory, and in those cases the two frameworks are rival models for recognition memory. Both systems are based on categorical data, i.e., multinomial data. Both measurement frameworks have important parameters to measure the quality of memory at the time of test, and both frameworks provide a method for correcting for subject response biases.Although the receiver operator characteristic (ROC) curves are not equivalent for the two approaches, these curves can look so similar that it is challenging to discriminate between the two frameworks (more will be devoted to this topic later). 
                         1 
                      
                      
                         1 
                         In this paper the ROC is taken to be the plot of the hit rate versus the false alarm rate, and the z-ROC is taken to be a plot of the linearizing transformations of the hit and false alarm rates. 
                       Despite the high degree of similarity of these two measurement frameworks, there is one potential difference between the frameworks, and this difference is linked to the theoretical representation of foil items. In this paper some new formal results from hazard function analysis and reverse hazard function analysis are used to assess the two theoretical frameworks for recognition memory by means of a detailed examination of foil items.

In this paper strength-based and selective MPT alternatives for recognition memory are briefly reviewed in Section   2 . Also in this section, the topic of mixture processes is discussed and linked to some of the differences between the memory measurement systems. In Section   3 , the general topic of mixture detection is discussed in terms of density and hazard methods. In Section   4 , several new theorems are developed that provide a new technique for the detection of mixtures. Also in this section the statistical issues for applying the new methods are discussed. In Section   5 , the results from a number of experiments are examined with the new theoretical tools for uncovering mixtures. Finally, in Section   6 , the implications of the new results are discussed within the broader context of recognition memory phenomena.

The memory for an event is represented differently in strength-based models and MPT models. The different characterizations of information parallel an important distinction drawn in psychophysics between  prothetic  and  metathetic  continua ( Stevens, 1957, 1961 ).  Stevens (1961, p. 41)  observed the following distinction about loudness (a prothetic continuum) and pitch (a metathetic continuum): 
                            …  it is interesting that some of the better known prothetic continua seem to be mediated by an  additive  mechanism at the physiological level, whereas the metathetic continua appear to involve  substitutive  processes at the physiological level. Thus we experience a change in loudness when excitation is added to excitation already present on the basilar membrane, but we note a change in pitch when new excitation is substituted for excitation that has been removed, i.e., the pattern of excitation is displaced

The Steven’s distinction stresses the inherent difference between changes in intensity and changes in qualities. Clearly pitch and hue are a frequency issue as a physicist looks at it; whereas, loudness and brightness are an amplitude issue. There can be graduated changes in either frequency or amplitude from a physical perspective. Stevens, however, is stressing the point that the physiological responses are quite different in the two cases. For example, in the  Békésy (1960)  place theory for hearing, the frequency of a sound corresponds to the specific hair cell detectors that are activated—different frequencies activate different hair cell detectors. However, the loudness of a sound at some specific frequency is captured by the number of hair cells activated at that given frequency and by the firing rate of those hair cells. Hence, loudness has this additive property but frequency is characterized by the specific detectors activated. Also for some metathetic attributes, there is not an order associated with the attribute. For example, hues are not perceived as an ordered dimension, but brightness is clearly additive, and brightnesses can be ordered. The difference in the intensity of a prothetic dimension and the qualitative difference of metathetic stimuli parallels roughly the differences between a strength-based memory measurement framework and the MPT approach to memory measurement. The strength approach treats the memory of an encoded event in terms of a prothetic continuum; whereas MPT memory models choose to measure the probability that the metathetic, informational content of the event is sufficiently preserved in memory. In SDT, stimuli are characterized as an intensity change (e.g., foils are weak and targets are stronger by an additive amount). The differences among the various targets are largely neglected with the SDT approach. Any difference among the stimuli would have to be in terms of strength, so in principle with the SDT approach, the target stimuli have an ordering. However, with the MPT approach, there is specific and different informational content initially encoded for each stimulus. The stimuli on a target list are different from one another like the various colors are different.

Let us consider first the rationale for the MPT framework. A memory target has rich informational content, which includes the perceptually detected information as well as the cognitive elaborations of the target event. Even within the narrow class of stimuli that are typical of a well controlled memory experiment, the encodings are widely varied and qualitatively different. For example, consider a consonant triad target, such as LRH. Clearly the individual letters and their position within the triad are distinctly different in terms of informational content. Moreover, individuals can cognitively elaborate the stimulus in very different ways. Based on post-experimental interviews,  Chechile (1973)  made note of some of these encoding strategies. For example, one subject reported that she pictured each briefly presented auditory triad as letters being written on a blackboard. Another subject associated each stimulus with an item in his company’s stock inventory. One subject reported that LRH was encoded as “Linda Rides Horses” along with an image of a person on a horse. Other subjects kept the memory encoding at the auditory level. The subjects also reported some variability from trial-to-trial in their encoding strategy.

Because the target event is a complex pattern of information that is encoded in an idiosyncratic fashion, MPT modelers have chosen to measure the surviving memory at the time of test in terms of probabilities over some mutually exclusive categories ( Batchelder, Hu, & Riefer, 1994; Batchelder & Riefer, 1980; Chechile, 2004; Chechile & Meyer, 1976; Chechile, Sloboda, & Chamberland, 2012; Chechile & Soraci, 1999 ). Although there are important differences among these specific MPT models, each model conceptualizes target storage into mutually exclusive categories.

For some of the above MPT models, the information about the target event is either sufficiently stored, or it is deficient in some fashion, so even if the subject has access to the deficient information, it would not be enough to support the correct recall of the entire target. 
                               2 
                            
                            
                               2 
                               The recall test context is one where the subject must produce the item from memory without the benefit of seeing the item again. It is assumed that targets are complex, so the probability of a correct recall given incomplete storage is approximately zero. 
                             Targets in the sufficiently stored category could be encoded in many different ways, but in each case all the target information is still in the memory system. This two-part classification was used by  Batchelder and Riefer (1980) ,  Chechile (2004)  and  Chechile and Meyer (1976) . 
                               3 
                            
                            
                               3 
                               The  Batchelder and Riefer (1980)  model is based on free recall data of clusterable pairs. Although this model discussed storage and retrieval for pairs of items, it is not a model for recognition memory. It is included here because storage for a cluster is categorized as opposed to being treated as a continuous variable of strength. 
                             Items that are sufficiently stored might have variability in strength, but those differences are ignored with these MPT models because the storage is sufficient to support correct recall. Similarly, items that are insufficiently stored might have variability, but these differences are also ignored. The two mutually exclusive categories of sufficient storage versus insufficient storage are measured by the respective probabilities, i.e.,

For the  Chechile (2004)  and  Chechile and Meyer (1976)  models, it is assumed that on an old recognition test, the subject will be highly confident and correct if there is sufficient storage, but with insufficient storage there might be either a yes or no response, and the confidence might not be as high. These MPT models thus treat storage at the time of test as a mixture over two distinctly different states. One state of the memory representation supports correct recall if there is retrieval access, and it supports a confidence correct recognition response, but the other state does not result in correct recall and might lead to either unconfident or incorrect recognition.

Chechile and Soraci (1999)  achieved a three-part classification of the targets by using a different model and a different set of test protocols. 
                               4 
                            
                            
                               4 
                               The test protocols for this model first included a recall test that was followed by a contingent series of forced-choice recognition tests. 
                             This model decomposed the state of insufficient storage into two mutually exclusive categories–fractional storage and non-storage. In the context of test protocols for the  Chechile and Soraci (1999)  model, a case is made that some targets are indistinguishable from novel items; whereas other targets items lead to above-chance performance. Fractional storage is demonstrated by an error on an initial four-alternative forced choice test (4AFC) but with above-chance performance on the series of follow-up forced-choice tests. Thus with the  Chechile and Soraci (1999)  model, the quality of storage is a mixture over three mutually exclusive categories and results in probability measures for sufficient storage (

Recently  Chechile et al. (2012)  introduced a novel set of recognition protocols that enabled a four-part categorization. The recognition memory testing protocol for this model involved an initial yes/no decision based on a set of four items. Subjects were instructed to respond “yes” if any of the four items appeared earlier in the experiment. The four items were either all foils or there was a target along with three foils. There was also a three-point confidence rating for the yes/no decision. Furthermore, on target-present trials, the yes/no test was followed by a series of forced-choice tests that were like the  Chechile and Soraci (1999) forced-choice task. With the  Chechile et al. (2012)  model, there are probability estimates for the four mutually exclusive categories that correspond to explicit storage (

                               5 
                            
                            
                               5 
                               Explicit storage is considered as a sufficient storage and can support the recall of the target if the subject can access that representation in memory. Implicit storage is considered as a faint residue of the whole target, and this trace cannot support correct recall or confidence correct recognition, but can support correct detection on a four-alternative forced-choice task.

Perhaps the model that has been contrasted and discussed the most with strength-based models is the two-high-threshold (2HTM) model ( Bröder & Schütz, 2009 ). 
                               6 
                            
                            
                               6 
                               Earlier source monitoring MPT models also used the concept of high and low thresholds ( Batchelder et al., 1994; Bayen, Murnane, & Erdfelder, 1996 ). 
                             With this model there are three cognitive states for information. These states correspond to (1) the detection of a target leading to a yes response, (2) the detection of a foil leading to a no response and (3) an intermediate uncertain state where the subject can guess either yes or no. As with the above models, this MPT model measures item information by a parameter corresponding to the probability (

                            ) that an item exceeds the high threshold and a parameter corresponding to the probability ( 
                               
                                  
                                     p 
                                  
                                  
                                     n 
                                  
                               
                            ) that an item triggers foil detection.

All the MPT models discussed in this section have some common features. With each model there is a grouping of items into categories associated with important informational properties such as sufficient versus insufficient storage. In addition these MPT models are mixture models where the mixture is in terms of the informational status of the target, e.g. some items are sufficiently stored whereas other items are insufficiently stored. Also all of these models have nuisance parameters that effectively correct for other pertinent processes that are involved with the experimental task. Finally in each of these models, there is no attempt to posit the detailed informational content of the memory encoding. The format of the encoding at the time of test could be idiosyncratically variable across items and/or subjects, yet with the above MPT models, the memory items are grouped into definable categories that are measured with probability values.

Single-process SDT models

A strength-based approach to recognition treats the concept of familiarity as a prothetic continuum that takes on continuous values.  Egan (1958)  first observed that the target-present versus target-absent testing used in a recognition memory study parallels the signal-present versus signal-absent tests used in a sensory-based signal detection study. Hence, the mathematics developed for signal detection theory (SDT) in sensory psychology could be exported to recognition memory in order to measure the memory strength of the target. Signal detection theory itself emerged from the psychophysical context as a theory that (a) obtained a signal sensitivity measure separate from a measure of the decision criterion, and (b) measured the stimulus strength without using the concept of a sensory threshold ( Green & Swets, 1966; Tanner & Swets, 1954 ).  Macmillan and Creelman (2005)  and  Wickens (2002) discuss the general technical details associated with signal detection theory as it applies to either a sensory or a recognition memory application. From the SDT framework, target items are represented in terms of a unimodal distribution on an axis associated with memory strength, and foil items are similarly represented by a different (and weaker) unimodal distribution on the strength axis. The normal distribution is the standard assumption for these latent distributions. Within the SDT framework, the specific content of memory is ignored, and the target is instead measured as a function of the difference between the strength values of the target and foil distributions. Thus the strength of the target is scaled relative to the strength of the foils. See  Fig. 1 
                             for an illustration of target and foil distributions along with five different decision points on the strength axis.

The SDT model also results in a predicted curvilinear ROC. Some of the evidence in support of the SDT model comes from the finding of nonlinear ROC curves ( Murdock, 1974 ). This finding was taken as evidence against a threshold theory of recognition memory, such as the  Blackwell (1963)  high-threshold model. The high-threshold model predicted a linear ROC with a nonzero  
                               y 
                            -axis intercept. Since this classic report, there has been an intensive discussion of recognition memory theories in terms of ROC curves. From the examination of linearized or z-ROC plots, there is also a considerable body of evidence developed that the standard deviation of the target distribution is larger than that of the foil distribution ( Ratcliff, McKoon, & Tindall, 1994; Ratcliff, Sheu, & Gronlund, 1992 ). The unequal-variance SDT model results in ROC curves that are not symmetric.

The dual process SDT model

There have been a number of dual process models for recognition, although it is the  Yonelinas (1994, 1997)  model that has attracted the most attention in the memory literature and is considered by most memory researchers as the “dual process” model. 
                               7 
                            
                            
                               7 
                               There are an assortment of other models that identified two processes in recognition memory (e.g.,   Atkinson & Juola, 1974; Chechile, 2004; Chechile & Meyer, 1976; DeCarlo, 2002; Jacoby, Toth, & Yonelinas, 1993; Mandler, 1980 ). The  Atkinson and Juola (1974)  model employed two thresholds on the usual signal-detection framework of two overlapping, continuous distributions for a model with the focus on accounting for the speed of the recognition decision. The  Chechile (2004)  and the  Chechile and Meyer (1976)  models were MPT mixture models for both target-present and target-absent tests. The  DeCarlo (2002)  model is a generic mixture that is not limited to either (a) recognition memory or (b) specific processes. The  Jacoby et al. (1993)  model dealt with the process dissociation model for separating explicit and implicit memory.  Mandler (1980)  also hypothesized a dual-process model where there was an initial familiarity process followed by a more deliberative secondary decision process; whereas in the Yonelinas dual-process model, the initial process was that of recollection followed by a familiarity decision for the case that the target was not recovered via recollection. 
                            
                            Yonelinas (1994, 1997)  argued for two different processes in old recognition and a single process in new recognition. The two processes in old recognition are familiarity and recollection; whereas in foil recognition there is only a familiarity process.

Yonelinas (1994)  assumed that for target-present tests there is a mixture of two processes, i.e., on some trials the subject is able to recollect the target information but if that process fails, then the subject can still respond “yes” correctly based on the strength of the familiarity elicited by the target item presented at test. The probability for the recollective process is denoted as  
                               
                                  
                                     R 
                                  
                                  
                                     o 
                                  
                               
                            . For the  
                               1 
                               − 
                               
                                  
                                     R 
                                  
                                  
                                     o 
                                  
                               
                             proportion of the trials where there is not target recollection, the model is identical to the equal variance case of SDT. In essence this version of the dual-process model for old recognition test trials is a hybrid of a MPT model and a SDT model. However, on foil test trials, the  Yonelinas (1994)  dual-process model is the same as a pure SDT model for foil trials. Consequently, there are two mechanisms (recollection and familiarity) for old recognition tests but only a single process (familiarity) for foil recognition tests.

The  Yonelinas (1997)  version of the dual-process model is the same as the  Yonelinas (1994)  model for item testing, but there is a different model when testing paired associates. Both a recollective and familiarity process are used for both old and new recognition in the  Yonelinas (1997)  pairs-testing model.  Yonelinas (1997)  provides equations for the hit rate and the false alarm rate for paired-associate testing. Interestingly the equations are the same as the corresponding equations for the 2HTM model discussed previously. 
                               8 
                            
                            
                               8 
                               It is also the case that the dual-process model for pairs is essentially the same as Model 3 from  Chechile and Meyer (1976) . For both the  Yonelinas (1997)  pairs model and the  Chechile and Meyer (1976)  Model 3, there is the same rate for the mixture between the knowledge state and the guessing state on target test trials as there is on foil test trials. 
                             Consequently, the  Yonelinas (1997)  version of the dual-process is a hybrid model in a number of ways. It is a MPT model for the case of pair testing. However, it is a hybrid of a strength model and a MPT model when testing for items on old recognition, but a pure strength model for foil trials.

Issues debated among strength-based theorists

Given the hybrid nature of the dual-process model, it is not surprising that there has been vigorous debates among strength-based theorists. There have been a number of papers that have reviewed the strength-based approaches to recognition memory ( Diana, Reder, Arndt, & Park, 2006; Dunn, 2004; Malmberg, 2008; Wixted & Stretch, 2004; Yonelinas, 2002 ). These papers have devoted considerable attention to the issue of the necessity of including multiple processes in recognition memory.

One of the arguments in support of the dual-process account is the direct evidence of a recollective response in yes/no recognition testing—in particular the research on the remember/know experimental paradigm ( Gardiner & Richardson-Klavehn, 2000; Tulving, 1985 ). The remember/know paradigm consists of a metacognitive judgment from the subjects. Following a “yes” response, the subjects are instructed either to report “remember” if they recollect the target event or report “know” if they have a sense of familiarity that the target is old. This experimental paradigm has generated a considerable body of experiments that have been the source of a spirited debate among memory theorists using strength models ( Diana et al., 2006; Dunn, 2004; Wixted & Stretch, 2004 ). 
                               9 
                            
                            
                               9 
                               It should be pointed out that there is also a body of experiments that have used an experimental paradigm that was first introduced by  Chechile and Meyer (1976) . The Chechile–Meyer paradigm consisted of an intermixing of recognition test trials with actual recall trials. The experiments that used the Chechile–Meyer paradigm typically find evidence for non-zero levels of recall. Those experiments were examined in terms of a storage/retrieval separation model, but the experiments also support the claims that recognition is a mixture of occasions where the subject can recollect the critical event along with occasions where other recognition decisional processes are used to render a yes/no judgment. 
                             With the focus on the remember-know paradigm,  Donaldson (1996)  argued that a single process SDT memory with a criterion for a remember response versus a know response could account for the remember-know findings. This point has been also supported by  Dunn (2004) . Yet  Diana et al. (2006)  argue the contrary position that the remember-know paradigm supports the claim that there are multiple processes involved in recognition testing. Still another view for the remember-know paradigm is provided by  Rotello, Macmillan, and Reeder (2004) . The  Rotello et al. (2004)  model is based on a two-dimensional strength representation with different multidimensional decision criteria for remember and know judgments. The two strength dimensions are specific strength and global strength.

Finally, standard SDT and the dual-process models have both been criticized by memory theorists who are focused on specific process models of recognition. For example,  Malmberg (2008)  discusses integrated process models such as the REM model by  Shiffrin and Steyvers (1997)  as an alternative to the dual-process model and the standard SDT model. However, the current set of process models is not designed to render measurements for a single experimental condition. Rather these models are identified over a set of conditions, and as such these models of memory will not be considered in this paper as memory measurement models.

Beyond the differences in the mathematical representation, the MPT memory measurement models and the strength-based models also differ in their goals. For strength-based models, there has been a primary focus on examining the properties of the yes/no recognition memory as exhibited by the ROC curve. By embracing a signal detection framework, the researcher implicitly assumes that the content of memory remains available and that the changes in memory retention are only reflected by changes in the strength of the target. However, MPT models have focused on the information processing constructs of storage and retrieval ( Batchelder & Riefer, 1980; Chechile, 2004; Chechile & Meyer, 1976 ).  Melton (1963, p. 4) , for example, remarked, “What, then, are the principal issues in a theory of memory? These are about either the storage or the retrieval of traces”. The measures of recognition memory obtained from a SDT approach do not measure the probability of storage of a complex pattern, nor do they measure the probability of retrieving stored memory traces. When memory changes across experimental conditions, the difference between the conditions is measured in MPT models in terms of storage and retrieval changes rather than assuming that all the traces are stored and attributing the changes to either strength or decision criteria differences. Moreover, because the focus of MPT models is on storage and retrieval processes, the necessary data collected for some models is not even yes/no recognition data. For example, the  Batchelder and Riefer (1980)  model is designed for the recall of clustered pairs and the recall of singleton items. Also the  Chechile and Soraci (1999)  model is designed for a recall task followed by a series of forced-choice recognition tests.

Given the very different measurement goals for the MPT approach for modeling recognition memory, it is not surprising that the ROC curve is not a particularly important objective from the MPT perspective. Nonetheless, there has been considerable attention on the ROC curve by strength theorists as a means to discriminate between existing strength models and selective MPT models. In particular, the single threshold  Blackwell (1963)  model and the two-high threshold model ( Bröder & Schütz, 2009 ) predict a linear untransformed ROC between the threshold extrema points. The detection of curvature between the extrema points is taken as evidence against the MPT approach for yes/no recognition memory (e.g.   Rotello et al., 2004; Wixted, 2007 ). However, the interior portion of the ROC curve is primarily a measure of response bias, and as such response bias is handled by secondary (nuisance parameters) rather than by key memory storage measures. Among the MPT models for yes/no recognition, there are differences as to how guessing and confidence judgments are treated. Many different shapes for the ROC curve are possible between the extrema points. In the next subsection this point will be illustrated by a more detailed discussion of the multiple-state (MS) MPT model.

Although some researchers have rejected some MPT models based on an examination of the ROC curve (e.g.   Rotello et al., 2004; Wixted, 2007 ), the MPT model class is very broad, so it is possible to have a MPT model that has a wide range of possibilities for the ROC curve. As an example, consider the MPT model illustrated in  Fig. 2 
                          (the old recognition tree) and  Fig. 3 
                          (the new recognition tree). The MPT model is briefly described in the figure captions. The model is designed for a yes/no recognition task where the subject also must provide a three-point confidence judgment. 
                            10 
                         
                         
                            10 
                            Typically studies from my laboratory provide verbal anchors for the confidence rating, i.e., the subjects are instructed to use the “3” rating if they are certain of their response, use the “2” rating for an educated guess, and use the “1” rating for a blind guess. 
                          From a SDT perspective this task results in a six-point ordinal scale that increases in the degree of targetness, i.e., “no 3”, “no 2”, “no 1”, “yes 1”, “yes 2”, and “yes 3”. For simplicity let us denote these categories as N3, N2, N1, Y1, Y2, and Y3. These are the six categories illustrated in  Fig. 1 . This ordering is not from a MPT modeling perspective, rather it is from a signal detection framework.

The key point here is not what the slope components are for the MS model because those slopes are primarily properties of the nuisance guessing and confidence rating parameters. From a MPT modeling perspective, the nuisance parameters are of interest in order to correct for conditional processes that occur when there is an absence of target information. The goal of the MS model is the measurement of the key unconditional parameters that deal with (a) the probability of sufficient target storage ( 
                            
                               
                                  θ 
                               
                               
                                  S 
                               
                            
                         ) and (b) the probability of recovered target information on foil trials ( 
                            
                               
                                  θ 
                               
                               
                                  k 
                               
                            
                         ). For the MS model some targets at the time of test are sufficiently stored in memory whereas the rest are insufficiently stored. This first branch for target test trials is a stochastic mixture model. Also for the MS model there is a mixture process for foil test trials. Some foils (at the time of test) can be rejected because the subject knows enough information about the targets to reject those foils with high confidence.

Given the flexibility of the MPT model class for handling various shapes of the ROC curve, how can MPT and strength models be tested? One property of the MPT models is that these models directly measure the mixing rates between different states for target information. Measures, such as  
                            
                               
                                  θ 
                               
                               
                                  S 
                               
                            
                          and  
                            
                               
                                  θ 
                               
                               
                                  k 
                               
                            
                          of the MS model, are mixing parameters. For example,  
                            
                               
                                  θ 
                               
                               
                                  S 
                               
                            
                          is the proportion of the targets that are in a state of sufficient storage; whereas  
                            1 
                            − 
                            
                               
                                  θ 
                               
                               
                                  S 
                               
                            
                          is the proportion of the targets that are insufficiently stored (either fractionally stored or not stored at all). Similarly,  
                            
                               
                                  θ 
                               
                               
                                  k 
                               
                            
                          is the proportion of the foil test trials where the targets are retrieved in enough detail to reject the test probe confidently, and  
                            1 
                            − 
                            
                               
                                  θ 
                               
                               
                                  k 
                               
                            
                          is the proportion of the test trials where there is a default guessing process in the absence of specific target information.

Unlike the MPT approach, the standard SDT strength model does not involve a mixture for either target-present or target-absent tests. For the  Yonelinas (1994)  dual-process model, there is a mixture process for old recognition tests but not for foil items. In the case of the  Yonelinas (1997)  model, there is a mixture process for both targets and foils when testing is done for paired associates, but if there is item testing, then the foil items are not considered as a mixture. Consequently, item foil testing is only considered as a mixture process for some of the MPT models discussed earlier. As a result, a critical test for recognition memory models can be achieved with the assessment of the mixture issue for foil items. If there were evidence of a mixture for foil items, then that evidence would be consistent with a MPT measurement approach, but it would not be consistent with the existing strength model for recognition memory. This paper is primarily focused on this critical question about foil test trials.

A case will be made that hazard function analysis is a powerful method for detecting the presence of a mixture in general, but before examining how memory hazard functions can be used to tackle this issue, it is necessary to first examine some basic ideas about hazard and reverse hazard. In the next section, the basis for using hazard functions as a tool for mixture detection is developed.

Detecting the presence of a stochastic mixture is challenging. To illustrate the problem, consider density plots for two cases where there is a mixture of two normal components. For each case the normal components have means located at 0 and 3 on the strength axis. The standard deviation is 1 for both normally distributed components of the mixture. For one case the mixing rate for the two components is .95 versus .05 for the respective components. For the second case the two components are weighted equally. The density displays for these two cases are shown in  Fig. 5 
                      . This example shows that the detection of a mixture of even widely separated components is difficult when the mixing rates are unequal. Also mixtures are difficult to detect, with equal mixing rates, if the distributions are not widely separated.

Hazard functions and reverse hazard functions can play an important role in detecting stochastic mixtures. However, before addressing this topic, some basic information about hazard and reverse hazard needs to be described.

The context for a hazard function or for a reverse hazard function is an ordered dimension or ordered set of discrete outcomes. For a continuous probability distribution, the following formal definitions are given below for hazard and reverse hazard.

Often hazard and reverse hazard has been applied to cases where there is a state change. For such cases, both hazard and reverse hazard are conditional probability densities for a state change occurring at a particular value for  
                            x 
                         , say  
                            
                               
                                  x 
                               
                               
                                  0 
                               
                            
                         . For hazard, the conditioning is on the fact that the state change did not occur for  
                            x 
                            < 
                            
                               
                                  x 
                               
                               
                                  0 
                               
                            
                         , but for reverse hazard, the conditioning is on the fact that the state change did occur for  
                            x 
                            ≤ 
                            
                               
                                  x 
                               
                               
                                  0 
                               
                            
                         . Hazard functions have been widely used in psychology since their introduction to the field by  Townsend and Ashby (1978, 1983) . See  Chechile (2003, 2006) ,  Luce (1986) , and  Townsend and Ashby (1978, 1983)  for a collection of theorems and other theoretical facts about hazard functions. More recently the concept of reverse hazard has been introduced into the psychological literature ( Townsend & Wenger, 2004 ). See  Chechile (2011)  for a review of reverse hazard and for a set of useful mathematical results that apply for reverse hazard.

For continuous probability distributions, there is a class of functions, called PF 2  functions, that have known hazard and reverse hazard properties ( Chechile, 2003, 2011 ). 
                            11 
                         
                         
                            11 
                            PF 2  refers to Pólya functions of order 2 ( Schoenberg, 1951 ). In general this class of functions need not be restricted to probability density functions, but  Chechile (2003, 2011)  showed if the functions were a probability density function, then important hazard and reverse hazard properties resulted. 
                          The definition of this functional class is provided below.

From  Theorem 3 , it is established that the hazard and reversal hazard are monotonic for a normal distribution, i.e., monotonically increasing hazard and monotonically decreasing reverse hazard. However, the monotonicity pattern can be altered if there is a mixture of normal distributions with different centers for the mixture components. In the next two theorems, the hazard and reverse hazard are respectively examined for a mixture of two components where each component has a normal distribution.

                          It follows from  Theorem 4  that the characteristic hazard function for a mixture of two normal distributions with sufficiently separated means is an IPVI profile where I, P, and V correspond respectively to Increasing, Peak, and Valley. This IPVI profile for a stochastic mixture of two separated components that individually have increasing hazard is not a unique property of Gaussian distributions.  Chechile (2003)  also found this characteristic IPVI profile resulted from a two-component mixture of logistic distributions that are sufficiently separated components, as well as for a mixture of separated Gumbel distributions (see Tables 1 and 2 from   Chechile, 2003 ). Both logistic and Gumbel distributions have monotonically increasing hazard when there is not a mixture of components. Also a  
                            
                               
                                  χ 
                               
                               
                                  2 
                               
                            
                          distribution with degrees of freedom  
                            ν 
                            > 
                            2 
                          has a monotonically increasing hazard because it is a PF 2  function; however, a mixture of  
                            
                               
                                  χ 
                               
                               
                                  2 
                               
                            
                          distributions has a IPVI hazard profile provided that there is sufficient difference in the degrees of freedom for the individual components of the mixture. 
                            12 
                         
                         
                            12 
                            For example, it is easy to show with a simple R program that a 50/50 mixture of two  
                                  
                                     
                                        χ 
                                     
                                     
                                        2 
                                     
                                  
                                distributions with degrees of freedom of 3 and 10 results in an IPVI hazard function. The mean for a component is equal to the degrees of freedom for the component. 
                          A Weibull distribution with shape parameter  
                            c 
                            > 
                            1 
                          is well known to have an increasing hazard function, but a mixture of sufficiently spaced Weibull components has an IPVI hazard profile. 
                            13 
                         
                         
                            13 
                            The Weibull density function is  
                                  f 
                                  
                                     ( 
                                     x 
                                     ) 
                                  
                                  =

                                        b

                                           )

                                        1 
                                     
                                  
                                  exp

                                  x

                                  0 
                                where  
                                  c 
                                is the shape parameter and the  
                                  b 
                                parameter is the scale factor. A R program was written to demonstrate that an IPVI hazard profile results for a 50/50 mixture of Weibull components that individually have a shape parameter of 2 provided that there is a sufficient difference in the scale factor parameter for the components. For example, an IPVI hazard function results if one Weibull component has parameters  
                                  c 
                                  = 
                                  2

                           The Ex-Gaussian distribution is yet another probability model that is known to have monotonically increasing hazard ( Luce, 1986 ), but a stochastic mixture of different Ex-Gaussian distributions can also result in IPVI hazard profile. 
                            14 
                         
                         
                            14 
                            The Ex-Gaussian distribution is the convolution of independent random variables  
                                  
                                     V 
                                  
                                and  
                                  
                                     W 
                                  
                                where  
                                  
                                     V 
                                  
                                is distributed as an exponential pdf with a parameter  
                                  τ 
                               , and  
                                  
                                     W 
                                  
                                is distributed as a Gaussian pdf with mean  
                                  μ 
                                and variance  
                                  
                                     
                                        σ 
                                     
                                     
                                        2 
                                     
                                  
                               . Given  
                                  τ 
                                  = 
                                  1 
                                and  
                                  σ 
                                  = 
                                  1 
                               , a 50/50 mixture of Ex-Gaussian distributions has an IPVI hazard profile provided that the means of the Gaussian random variable for the components are separated by more than 3.3. 
                          Currently there is no known theorem that generally establishes an IPVI hazard profile for a stochastic mixture of two components that individually have increasing hazard, provided that the component means are sufficiently separated. However, there is also no known exception for the IPVI hazard profile for a mixture of spaced components. All the continuous probability distributions that were discussed in  Luce (1986) , which have monotonically increasing hazard, result in an IPVI hazard profile for a spaced two-component mixture. Consequently, the IPVI hazard profile is a strong signal that a stochastic mixture is present because it is not a pattern exhibited by any known single-component distribution, but it is a typical pattern exhibited by a two-component stochastic mixture.

See the  Appendix  for the proof.  ■

The DVIPD reverse hazard profile is not uniquely associated with two-component Gaussian mixtures.  Chechile (2011)  showed that the mixture of logistic distributions changed from a monotonically decreasing reverse hazard to a DVIPD reverse hazard profile when the components of the mixture were beyond a critical separation. The transition from a monotonically decreasing reverse hazard function to a DVIPD reversal hazard profile also occurs for  
                            
                               
                                  χ 
                               
                               
                                  2 
                               
                            
                          mixtures, Gumbel mixtures, and Ex-Gaussian mixtures, provided that the components of the mixture are sufficiently separated. Thus, the DVIPD reverse hazard profile is a signal that a stochastic mixture is present because it is not a pattern exhibited by any known single-component distribution, but it is a pattern typically exhibited by a two-component stochastic mixture.

Because the shape of the hazard and the reverse hazard functions are different for mixtures in comparison to the homogeneous normal model, these functions can be used to detect a mixture. However, the components of the mixtures must be separate beyond a critical point or else the mixture will go undetected. In order to see the critical separation between two normally distributed components, a number of cases were examined via Mathematica. The results of these calculations are provided in  Table 1

But for more extreme splits in the mixing rate between the components than a 64%–36% split, then either hazard or reverse hazard requires a smaller critical separation than the density critical separation.

Although the continuous hazard and reverse hazard properties of the latent strength distribution for foil items is a central focus for this paper, it is clear that continuous hazard is not directly measured in a recognition memory experiment. The data from a memory experiment is categorical, i.e., responses in categories such as the six responses illustrated in  Fig. 1 . For applications with discrete response categories, such as in recognition memory experiments, we need to deal with discrete hazard  
                            
                               
                                  h 
                               
                               
                                  k 
                               
                            
                          and discrete reverse hazard  
                            
                               
                                  r 
                               
                               
                                  k 
                               
                            
                         , which were respectively defined in  Definitions 3 and 4 . The key to linking the hazard properties of a continuous distribution to discrete distributions generated from the continuous probability distribution is contained in several important lemmas that are provided below.

The linkage between the cumulative probability and hazard is shown in the next lemma.

In Section   2  a case was made that a critical test for classes of recognition memory models centers about the question of mixtures for foil items. In Section   3  we saw how hazard and reverse hazard can be different when there are mixtures present in the data. However, there are some issues that must be handled in order to apply hazard methods to recognition memory data. For example, it is easy to compute the discrete hazard for memory data for a yes–no recognition decision that is accompanied with a three-point confidence rating, like the data shown in  Fig. 1 . However, the properties of the discrete hazards are not equivalent to the hazard properties of the theoretical continuous distribution. To illustrate this point, consider the example where the theoretical continuous distribution is the standard normal, and the criteria values  
                            
                               
                                  x 
                               
                               
                                  1 
                               
                            
                         ,  
                            
                               
                                  x 
                               
                               
                                  2

                            … 
                         ,  
                            
                               
                                  x 
                               
                               
                                  5 
                               
                            
                          are respectively −1, 0, .1, .4, and 1. These decision points result in the discrete distribution for  
                            
                               
                                  p 
                               
                               
                                  1 
                               
                            
                         ,  
                            
                               
                                  p 
                               
                               
                                  2 
                               
                            
                         ,  
                            … 
                         ,  
                            
                               
                                  p 
                               
                               
                                  6 
                               
                            
                          of.1587,.3413,.0398,.1156,.1859, and .1587. The corresponding discrete hazard values  
                            
                               
                                  h 
                               
                               
                                  1 
                               
                            
                         ,  
                            
                               
                                  h 
                               
                               
                                  2 
                               
                            
                         ,  
                            … 
                         ,  
                            
                               
                                  h 
                               
                               
                                  6 
                               
                            
                          from  (21)  are.1587,.4057,.0796,.2512,.5396, and 1. Note that the discrete hazard distribution increases from the first to the second value then drops to a lower value for the third point and then increases for the last three hazard values. Yet it is well established that the standard normal distribution has a strictly increasing hazard function (see  Theorem 3 ). However, the discrete hazards are a function of not only the underlying continuous distribution but also of the spacing of the decision points  
                            
                               
                                  x 
                               
                               
                                  1 
                               
                            
                         ,

                                  2

                         ,  
                            
                               
                                  x

                         . With other arrangements for the decision points, the discrete hazard can exhibit many possible patterns. Yet theoretical interest is concerned with the hazard properties of the underlying continuous distribution rather than the empirical discrete hazard profile. How then can information about the continuous hazard function be extracted from discrete hazard values?

Information about mean continuous hazard within categorical boundaries can be obtained from recognition memory experiments where the subjects have ordinal categories for their responses as illustrated in  Fig. 1 . For example, the least target-like response would be “no 3” and would correspond to the probability below the decision point  
                            
                               
                                  x 
                               
                               
                                  1

The reason for this limitation is because the theorem relies on using the intermediate or mean value theorem from integral calculus, which in turn requires that the intervals be finite. Apart from this limitation,  Theorem 6  is quite general because it does not require the specification of a particular underlying distribution for the target-present or target-absent conditions. The distributions can have any sort of hazard property, and  Theorem 6  still applies.

There is a long-standing consensus in the SDT memory modeling approach that the underlying distributions are normal distributions. If the underlying probability distribution is assumed to be a normal distribution for the case of

For a monotonically increasing hazard function, like that of the normal distribution, all the hazard values for the next interval are larger than those from the previous interval. Consequently, the mean hazard for any given interval must be larger than the mean hazard from the preceding interval. If it is empirically established that there is a decrease in the mean hazard in a succeeding interval, then there would be evidence against the assumption of a normal distribution. Yet a decrease in some succeeding interval is exactly what is expected if the latent distribution involves a mixture of components that are separated by more than two standard deviation units.

The next theorem provides the method for finding the mean reverse hazard for regions of the continuous latent distribution.

If the underlying probability distribution is assumed to be a normal distribution, then it follows for case of  
                            b

For a monotonically decreasing reverse hazard function, like that of the normal distribution, all the reverse hazard values for the next interval are less than those from the previous interval. If it is empirically established that there is an increase in the mean reverse hazard in a succeeding interval, then there would be evidence against the assumption of the single-component normal distribution.

Obtaining an empirical hazard function or a reverse hazard function is challenging for a continuous distribution. A key problem is associated with the fact that hazard for a continuous variable is a ratio of two quantities that can become increasingly small as  
                            x 
                          increases in value. Reverse hazard has a similar problem for small values for  
                            x 
                         . Statistical error and bias are also possible with brute-force hazard or reverse hazard estimates (see   Chechile, 2003; Klein & Moeschberger, 1997 ). However, the mean hazard and mean reverse hazard in an interval by using Eqs.  (26)  and  (30)  is less challenging because ratios of cumulative proportions of multinomial data are not statistically problematic. Consequently, a feasible method for computing the mean hazard and mean reverse hazard is possible. Nonetheless, there are still a number of important methodological and statistical issues that need to be resolved in order to obtain meaningful estimates.

The first issue concerns the basis upon which the analysis of hazard is computed. Given that the focus of the analysis deals with the differences between a multinomial approach versus a signal-detection approach, it is essential that the mean hazard be computed on an individual basis. The alternative basis is to use data pooled across participants. But if the hazard is not monotonically increasing for the pooled analysis, then the mixture detected might be interpreted as simply a mixture of different types of individuals. Therefore, in order to infer that a mixture is contrary to the signal detection theory model of recognition memory, it is necessary to examine the hazard on an individual basis. Moreover, the data for the individual should not be pooled over conditions that affect memory performance. If the data were pooled over conditions where there are memory retention differences, then the finding of a nonmonotonic hazard function could be interpreted as simply a mixture of weak and strong items. It is not necessary to assume that each item is equal in strength because SDT assumes strength variability that is distributed as a single component Gaussian distribution.

It is likely that the sample size is small when data are restricted to just the subject responses for approximately a homogeneous memory retention condition. A reduced sample size causes a number of problems for the estimation of mean hazard and reverse hazard via the respective equations of  (26)  and  (30) . For example, with cases like shown in  Fig. 1  where there are six response categories for target-present trials and six categories for target-absent trials, it is possible that there are categories with no responses. If maximum likelihood estimates are used for the cell proportions, then a zero in a cell results in a statistical estimate of zero and that in turn results in estimates of decision points that are equal. However, the mean hazard and reverse hazard are inversely proportional to the difference between the decision points. A zero difference due to decision points coinciding results in a divergent hazard estimate. Yet it is very improbable that a cell proportion in the population with unlimited test trials would be zero for any cell. Hence, the estimation of a cell proportion of zero is not a credible estimate, despite the fact it might be a maximum likelihood estimate. In part the problem is due to a limitation of the maximum likelihood method itself, especially in small sample size experiments ( Le Cam, 1986 , p. 622;  Stuart & Ord, 1991 , pp. 658–661).

The problems caused by a frequency count of zero in a response category is circumvented by the use of Bayesian estimation procedures. If one were to use a Bayesian posterior point estimate with an uninformed or diffuse prior probability, then the problem caused by a data category with a zero frequency count disappears. Given a multinomial with  
                            m 
                          categories, the Bayesian posterior mean estimate for the proportion in the  
                            j 
                         th category is  
                            
                               
                                  
                                     
                                        n 
                                     
                                     
                                        j 
                                     
                                  
                                  + 
                                  1 
                               
                               
                                  n 
                                  + 
                                  m 
                               
                            
                         , where  
                            
                               
                                  n 
                               
                               
                                  j 
                               
                            
                          is the outcome frequency for that category and  
                            n 
                          is the total frequency count for the multinomial (see  Chechile, 1998 , for a more extensive discussion of the Bayesian estimates of the multinomial cell proportions). The posterior modal estimate is  
                            
                               
                                  
                                     
                                        n 
                                     
                                     
                                        j 
                                     
                                  
                               
                               
                                  n 
                               
                            
                         , and is the same as the maximum likelihood estimate. Finally the posterior median estimate can be approximated as the mean of the other two estimates because for multinomial estimates the median is between the mean and the mode. Note that the Bayesian median estimate for a category proportion cannot be zero. Consequently, with a median Bayesian estimate for an individual, the problem of multinomial categories with no observations is circumvented. Nonetheless, it is still advantageous to reduce the problem of categories with low frequencies by having a suitably large sample size.

From a Bayesian perspective the optimal point estimate of a quantity can be either the posterior mode, median, or mean; the choice depends on which cost function that is minimized (see ( Bernardo & Smith, 1994; Chechile, 1977 )). The mode is the optimal estimate only if the cost of the estimator is a 0 or 1 function, i.e., no cost if the estimator is correct and a cost of 1 otherwise. The median of the posterior distribution is an optimal estimator that minimizes an absolute value cost function.

The actual estimates used a Bayesian Monte Carlo sampling method that is called population parameter mapping (PPM) ( Chechile, 1998, 2004, 2009, 2010a,b; Chechile et al., 2012 ). With the PPM approach, random vectors are drawn from the Bayesian posterior distributions for the two separate six-cell multinomials. The sampling distribution for the computed mean hazard is not known. Consequently, it is advantageous to use a nonparametric statistical method that only uses the rank information about any difference in the mean hazard or any difference in mean reverse hazard. The Wilcoxon signed ranks test ( Siegel & Castellan, 1988 ) is an excellent choice for a powerful nonparametric method when comparing two within-subjects conditions. In terms of pertinent data for assessing the memory hazard characteristics, there are a number of studies (or conditions within experiments) that satisfy the set of design characteristics discussed above in Section   4.2 . The design requirements are: (1) the utilization of yes/no recognition testing with at least three rating categories, (2) the availability of frequency information on an individual basis, (3) a sample size of at least 20 old recognition tests and 20 foil recognition tests per participant, and (4) a sample of at least 15 participants in order to enable the  
                            z 
                          statistic associated with the Wilcoxon signed ranks test. The first two design requirements are particularly limiting because many recognition memory experiments simply obtain a yes/no response, and because it is typical for researchers not to publish the frequency data for the separate participants. However, there are eight conditions from various studies conducted in my laboratory that meet the above four design criteria, and these conditions are listed in  Table 2 . The retention lag and the type of memory items are also shown in  Table 2 . Study F tested fourth-grade children, but the participants in all the other studies were college students. There was some grouping across within-subject conditions in studies D through G in order to achieve a larger sample size  
                            
                               
                                  n 
                               
                               
                                  r 
                               
                            
                         , but the grouping was only done if the participant did not have a notable difference in memory retention across the grouped conditions.

Studies A–E listed in  Table 2  used a Brown–Peterson methodology ( Brown, 1958; Peterson & Peterson, 1959 ). For each trial a single target was followed by a retention interval that was filled with a rehearsal preventing activity, and finally there was a test for the target item. During testing the subject first gave a yes/no judgment and then a confidence rating on a three-point scale. The subjects were instructed to use a “3” as the rating for a certain response, a “2” for the rating of an educated guess, and a “1” rating for a blind guess. For Study F the targets were word stimuli arranged in a 3×2 array. At test a single word was represented in either the same position in a 3×2 array (old recognition) or the probe word was presented in a different array position (foil recognition). Confidence ratings were obtained in the same fashion as the other studies. Study H was a paired-associate list learning experiment. Each pair consisted of a CVC nonsense trigram as the stimulus and a word as the response member. There was a 20 s delay after the presentation of the entire lists of pairs before the test phase with a mean delay between presentation and test of 88 s. The foil test pair consisted of a rearranged pair, i.e., if a pair for learning was “HUJ VOLUME”, then a foil test would be a pair like “HUJ CHAPTER” where the word “CHAPTER” was on the learning list but paired with a different stimulus. Again three-point confidence ratings were collected for each recognition test trial. Finally for all eight studies, a random  
                            
                               
                                  1 
                               
                               
                                  3 
                               
                            
                          of the test trials was a recall test.

The reverse hazard for a normal should be monotonically decreasing, so the finding of an increase in the reverse hazard is again evidence contrary to the hypothesis of a single-component normal distribution for the foil items, although these findings are not significant at the Bonferroni  
                            α

Consequently, it is not necessary to find symmetry between the hazard and reverse hazard estimates. For all the studies, other than Study F, the departure from the expected monotonicity pattern of a normal distribution is more pronounced for hazard. However, for Study F, there is not a monotonicity violation for hazard, but there is a marginally significant violation for reverse hazard.

One might be concerned that the above analysis was based on the assumption that the distribution of the targets was a single-component normal distribution. The  Yonelinas (1994, 1997)  dual process model hypothesizes that there is a stochastic mixture for the targets but not for foils. Perhaps the finding of evidence in favor of a mixture for foils was an artifact of the assumption that the target trials were just distributed as a single-component normal distribution. If there were a mixture for the targets, then the evidence in support of a foil mixture would be stronger. The assumption of a single-component normal for the targets in  Table 3  analysis weakened the effect of a foil mixture. Despite this weakening, the analysis in  Table 3  nonetheless demonstrated the presence of a mixture for foil items.
