Because the evidence provided in  Table 3  shows a significant departure from the predicted monotonicity for the mean hazards and mean reverse hazards, this outcome cannot depend on the scale factor for the decision points. Consequently, the evidence for a mixture for the foil items is a robust effect that is not dependent on the value of the standard deviation of the target distribution.

In addition to the evidence for a mixture for foil items obtained from hazard analysis, each of the studies identified in  Table 2  had further evidence of a mixture from recall data. Each of the studies used an experimental paradigm where there was a random intermixing of old recognition, new recognition, and recall trials. This procedure was done in order that subjects would not know until the time of test how the target was to be tested. This procedure was advanced in order to create a context where the type of memory encoding is the same for recall test trials as for recognition test trials. When participants anticipate a recognition test as opposed to a recall test, the performance is affected ( Balota & Neely, 1980; Hall, Grossman, & Elwood, 1976; Neely & Balota, 1981 ). But with the random intermixing of test trial types, the participants cannot develop a reliable expectancy as to which type of test is going to be used on any specific trial, so recall and recognition tests are based on the same encoding of the target information.

In  Table 5 
                          the mean recall rate and the corresponding 95% confidence interval for correct recall is provided for each of the eight studies listed in  Table 2 . Clearly there is a nonzero recall rate for each of the experiments. It is important to stress that the recall task for each of the studies listed in  Table 2  used difficult items for encoding that had a near zero chance of being correct via guessing. Complex items were used in order to justify the conclusion that a correct recall event could only result from the storage and retrieval of the target item and was not likely the result of guessing. Consequently, the nonzero correct recall rate provides evidence that the participants can (on occasion) reproduce a memory target entirely without the retrieval assistance of a recognition test probe. Had there been a foil test instead of a recall test, then there is no doubt that the participant would reject that item and would be highly confident of their response. Moreover, had the participant received an old recognition test instead of a recall test, then there is no doubt that the participant would have given a high confidence “yes” response. The nonzero recall rate indicates that there are some occasions where the false alarm rate is zero with a hit rate of one. Consequently, the limiting case of the most stringent criterion would not result in the hit rate and the false alarm rate being zero. The evidence for a nonzero recall rate means that the limiting case of a stringent decision criterion would result in the point  
                            
                               ( 
                               0 
                               , 
                               
                               v 
                               ) 
                            
                          on the ROC curve, where the hit rate  
                            v 
                          would be greater than or equal to the recall rate,  
                            R 
                            c 
                         . Moreover, in the limiting case of the most lenient decision criterion, the nonzero recall rate implies that participants would not have a false alarm rate of 1 because participants would know (on some occasions) what the memory target was and would be able to reject the foil probe. Hence, the limiting case for the most lenient criterion would be  
                            
                               ( 
                               c 
                               , 
                               
                               1 
                               ) 
                            
                         , where the false alarm rate  
                            c 
                          would be less than or equal to  
                            1 
                            − 
                            R 
                            c 
                         . Consequently, the limiting point for the most lenient condition would not be the  
                            
                               ( 
                               1 
                               , 
                               1 
                               ) 
                            
                          point. Of course this claim based on the nonzero recall rates is consistent with MPT models of recognition such as the MS model described earlier. Consequently, the nonzero correct recall rate provides additional evidence that the participants can (on occasion) retrieve target information on foil trials and use that information to reject the foil with high confidence. This high confidence “no” response does not occur because the foil fails to produce a strong enough activation but because the subject has enough target knowledge retrieved to reject the foil test probe. These recall data thus provide independent validation evidence for the foil mixture process that some MPT models have stressed. 
                            17 
                         
                         
                            17 
                            Besides the MS model, the three MPT models in the  Chechile (2004)  paper and the IES model in the  Chechile et al. (2012)  paper also have a  
                                  
                                     
                                        θ 
                                     
                                     
                                        k 
                                     
                                  
                                parameter for foil recognition.

The approach used in this paper does not focus of the examination of the goodness-of-fit of an empirical ROC, nor does it address the issue of the tradeoff between the goodness-of-fit and model complexity. Both SDT and recognition memory MPT models, like the MS model, can do a credible job of accounting for the ROC curve. 
                         18 
                      
                      
                         18 
                         However, a recent paper by  Kellen, Klauer, and Bröder (2013)  does assess individual ROC curves with model complexity statistics and finds a preference for a discrete-state MPT model rather than strength theory models. 
                       Instead the approach utilized is based on evaluating a critical difference between the memory measurement systems. The assessment focused on answering the question—do foil recognition test trials involve a stochastic mixture? Foil trials are catch trials to control for the analysis of old recognition tests trials, but the behavior of subjects on foil trials is modeled differently by strength theories and MPT models. Both the SDT model and the  Yonelinas (1994, 1997)  dual process model consider foil detection as a homogeneous process that is represented by a single-component Gaussian distribution on a strength axis. By using a method derived from several new theorems, evidence is provided from the examination of mean hazard and mean reverse hazard that foil trials involve a stochastic mixture. For these analyses, the SDT Gaussian model is assumed for the target distribution; yet this assumption leads to a result that is contradictory to the SDT Gaussian model for the foil trials. Another analysis based on the assumption of the  Yonelinas (1994, 1997)  dual process model for targets also resulted in the contradictory conclusion that the foils were not distributed as a single-component Gaussian distribution. In both cases the mean hazard and reverse hazard functions exhibited a pattern that is typical of a stochastic mixture and is inconsistent with the monotonicity expected for a single-component Gaussian distribution. The additional evidence of a nonzero recall rate adds further support to the conclusion that there is a stochastic mixture for the foil test trials. These data are consistent with the view that sometimes the subject is able to retrieve enough information about the presented targets to reject the foil item with high confidence. On other test trials, the subject is not able to recover information about the targets, so foil decision making is based on default guessing and confidence rating processes. MPT models, like the MS model, provide a straightforward way to account for this stochastic mixture.

Although the ROC was not directly assessed in the present paper, evidence was nonetheless provided that a critical feature of the predicted ROC from a strength theory perspective was not supported. For each of the studies in  Table 2  there was a random set of recall test trials, and the subjects demonstrated a recall rate  
                         R 
                         c 
                       above zero. Unlike the metacognitive “remember” judgment in the response-know procedure, which  Donaldson (1996)  and  Dunn (2004)  argued was due to a separate criterion within a SDT model, the subjects, in fact, reproduced the target correctly on some recall test trials (see  Table 5 ). Because the subjects could recall the entire target (that is unlikely to be guessed) on some proportion of the test trials, it is obvious that the intercept for the ROC would be  
                         
                            ( 
                            c 
                            , 
                            
                            1 
                            ) 
                         
                       for  
                         c 
                         ≤ 
                         1 
                         − 
                         R 
                         c 
                       rather than the SDT predicted point of  
                         
                            ( 
                            1 
                            , 
                            1 
                            ) 
                         
                       for the most lenient recognition decision criterion condition. 
                         19 
                      
                      
                         19 
                         The nonzero recall rate also means that in the most stringent decision condition the ROC would be  
                               
                                  ( 
                                  0 
                                  , 
                                  d 
                                  ) 
                               
                            ,  
                               d 
                               > 
                               R 
                               c 
                             rather than SDT predicted point of  
                               
                                  ( 
                                  0 
                                  , 
                                  0 
                                  ) 
                               
                            . However, since the dual-process model acknowledges the possibility of item recollection for old items but not for foils, the focus here is on the predicted  
                               
                                  ( 
                                  1 
                                  , 
                                  1 
                                  ) 
                               
                             point.

Individuals decidedly experience a wide range of encoding conditions in the course of a typical day. There can be shifts in alertness, interest, emotional engagement, and the complexity of the information encountered. Hence, it is not plausible to assume a common, single-component distribution on a strength axis across this wide range of contexts. Although this point would not be disputed by many researchers, the present examination of mixtures has demonstrated that mixtures are also present in a single experimental condition for individual subjects who are presented with well controlled stimuli that are carefully prepared to be homogeneous with regard to their potential for encoding. Some targets might have undergone a more robust destructive storage interference over the retention interval than experienced by other targets. On some foil test trials, the subjects might be able to remember enough target information to reject the foil—not because the foil elicits a weak strength response but because of their knowledge of the targets.

It is not a trivial modification of a strength model to include mixtures simultaneously for target and foil trials and still have a strength model. For example, consider the  Yonelinas (1997)  dual-process model for associates where there are mixtures for both target and foil test trials. The equations for the hit and false alarm rates for this model are identical to the corresponding equations for the two-high-threshold MPT model ( Bröder & Schütz, 2009 ). In essence, the signal-detection feature of the dual-process model was abandoned when foil trials also have a mixture. As another example, consider the  DeCarlo (2002)  mixture model for signal detection. This model is a general signal detection model and is not limited to only a recognition memory application. Although this model is a mixture SDT model, most of the discussion in that paper deals with mixtures for strictly the signal-present trials. Later in the  DeCarlo (2007)  paper, a model (called the Mixture 2 model) is developed that does deal with two-component mixtures for both targets and foils. However, the recent study by  Chechile et al. (2012)  indicates that there can be more than two mixture components for target-present tests.  Chechile et al. (2012)  developed a novel MPT model that divided the memory representation into four mutually exclusive subcategories of (1) explicit storage, (2) implicit storage, (3) fractional storage, and (4) non-storage. It is difficult to see how a SDT strength model of recognition memory can handle a four-part mixture for target-present trials and a two part mixture for foil trials. Yet MPT models, like the multiple-state (MS) model and the IES model by  Chechile et al. (2012) , already exist and can easily handle mixtures for both targets and foils.

There are additional advantages of the MS model for recognition (as well as other similar MPT models that have been advanced heretofore). In memory studies, the investigator is keenly interested in memory failures. Given forgetting, the question naturally arises as to whether the memory failure is (a) due to a problem in the memory representation or (b) due to a problem with the retrieval of information from memory. These distinctions originate from a basic information-processing framework, and these distinctions are critically important for understanding memory. In contrast, the strength measures from SDT are neither a probability of target storage nor a probability of information retrieval. Additional advantages of the MPT approach for modeling recognition memory are discussed in the next two sections.

If it were the case that the target-foil similarity influenced the measure of storage, then the probability of storage would only be relative to the foils used for testing. The same argument can be raised in regard to a signal detection framework, i.e., if  
                            
                               
                                  d 
                               
                               
                                  ′ 
                               
                            
                          varies with target-foil similarity, then the value of  
                            
                               
                                  d 
                               
                               
                                  ′ 
                               
                            
                          is meaningless without the specification of the foil set and its similarity to the targets.

Gerrein (1976)  examined target-foil similarity in a fashion that enables modeling from either the framework of the MS model or with SDT. The memory targets were word triads presented via the Brown–Peterson experimental paradigm. The word triad was presented for 2.5 s. Memory testing followed after a retention interval during which the participants repeated aloud digits that were presented at a rate of 2 digits per s. There were two separate groups of 30 participants, i.e., 30 in Experiment 1 and 30 in Experiment 2. The target items were identical between the two experiments. The only systematic difference between the two experiments was the similarity of the foils to the targets. The foils were either highly similar to the target or were dissimilar to the target. For example, in the case of the target of “butterfly wool glacier”, a highly similar foil would be either “butterfly glacier wool” or “butterfly ocean glacier”; whereas a dissimilar foil would be “toe letter vest”. Clearly the participants in the similar condition knew the foils were very close to the target, and the participants knew in the dissimilar condition that the foils were all different words because the similarity manipulation was done between groups. In Experiment 3, the similarity of the foils to the targets was examined with a within-subjects design. The key model parameter estimates for both the MS model and the signal detection model are provided in  Table 6 
                         .

The manipulation of similarity of the foil to the target did have a notable effect on the false alarm rate. The false alarm rate was zero in the dissimilar foil condition and was higher in the similar condition. The target storage parameter  
                            
                               
                                  θ 
                               
                               
                                  S 
                               
                            
                          of the MS model was not reliably different between the two similarity conditions, but the knowledge of the target in foil recognition parameter ( 
                            
                               
                                  θ 
                               
                               
                                  k 
                               
                            
                         ) was substantially larger in the dissimilar foil condition. This result is reasonable because not much information about the target needs to be recovered at the time of test when dissimilar foils are used. Consequently, the probability of successfully recovering knowledge of the target during foil recognition testing should be greater in the dissimilar condition. Importantly, however, the target storage measure is stable with regard to changes in the target-foil similarity.

In contradistinction to the MS model description of the  Gerrein (1976)  data, the signal detection measure  
                            
                               
                                  d 
                               
                               
                                  ′ 
                               
                            
                          values are dramatically altered by the degree of the similarity of the foils to the targets. In Experiment 1–2, the value of  
                            
                               
                                  d 
                               
                               
                                  ′ 
                               
                            
                          is more than 40% larger in the dissimilarity condition. In Experiment 3, the value of  
                            
                               
                                  d 
                               
                               
                                  ′ 
                               
                            
                          is 260% larger in the dissimilarity condition. This dependency on foil similarity is a very serious problem for the SDT approach to recognition memory and illustrates that the numeric value of  
                            
                               
                                  d 
                               
                               
                                  ′ 
                               
                            
                          is meaningless without the knowledge of the target-foil similarity. This problem with the signal detection approach is clearly due to representing the strength of the targets relative to the strength of the foils. If the foils become more similar to the targets, then the signal detection measure of strength must change. But why should the strength of the items that were presented be altered by the nature of items not previously presented to the participants? Consequently, the MPT approach to modeling not only provides an excellent model for mixtures, the MPT approach also has an advantage of measuring memory in a fashion that is stable with respect to the properties of the foils. In essence the successful explanation of the target-foil similarity effect in the MS model works because foil trials are treated as a stochastic mixture that is influenced by the target-foil similarity.

The mirror effect is another phenomenon that has been troublesome for the SDT model of recognition memory, but it can be readily explained in the MS model by a differential mixing rate between conditions. The mirror effect is a robust effect in recognition memory that has been explored by Glanzer and his colleagues (viz.,  Glanzer & Adams, 1985; Glanzer, Adams, & Iverson, 1991; Glanzer, Adams, Iverson, & Kim, 1993; Glanzer, Kim, & Adams, 1998 ). The mirror effect refers to the ordering of the means of the distributions on the signal detection strength axis for cases where there are experimental conditions that result in different levels of memory retention. Let us refer to one condition as the “strong” memory condition relative to another condition that will be called the “weak” condition. Not surprisingly, the strength distributions associated with the two types of targets are ordered such that the mean of the strong condition is shifted to the right of the weak targets. However, Glanzer and colleagues showed that the corresponding foils have the reversed order on the strength axis, i.e., the foil distribution for the strong condition is shifted to the left of the foil distribution for the weak condition. This effect is problematic for the standard SDT model of recognition memory because all of the foils are novel items. There is no reason why the strength distributions for the two foil distributions should be different. It would seem more plausible in the SDT framework to assume that all the nonpresented items originate from a common strength distribution.

From the perspective of the MS model, the foil trials are divided between occasions where there is target knowledge (with probability  
                            
                               
                                  θ 
                               
                               
                                  k 
                               
                            
                         ) and occasions without target knowledge (with probability  
                            1 
                            − 
                            
                               
                                  θ 
                               
                               
                                  k 
                               
                            
                         ). If the rate of target knowledge on foil recognition is different between the strong and weak conditions, then that difference in itself can result in a mirror effect. For example, suppose in the weak condition that  
                            
                               
                                  θ 
                               
                               
                                  k 
                               
                            
                            = 
                            0 
                          but in the strong condition  
                            
                               
                                  θ 
                               
                               
                                  k 
                               
                            
                            = 
                            . 
                            35 
                         . If we suppose further that the signal detection decision criteria  
                            
                               
                                  x 
                               
                               
                                  1 
                               
                            
                            , 
                            
                            
                               
                                  x 
                               
                               
                                  2 
                               
                            
                            , 
                            
                            … 
                            , 
                            
                            
                               
                                  x 
                               
                               
                                  5 
                               
                            
                          are respectively.8, 1.0, 1.3, 1.5, and 2, then the difference in the rate for knowledge-based processes would cause a leftward shift of .40 for the foil distribution for the strong condition. This example is only intended to illustrate that the mirror effect is expected whenever there are differences in the  
                            
                               
                                  θ 
                               
                               
                                  k 
                               
                            
                          parameter between foil conditions. 
                            20 
                         
                         
                            20 
                            Other nuisance parameters might also vary with the strength of the condition. 
                          Consequently, the mirror effect illustrates another advantage of the MPT approach of modeling recognition foil trials as a mixture because reasonable between-condition differences in the mixing rate can naturally account for the mirror effect which is otherwise a problematic effect from the SDT perspective.

In the previous section we have seen that the rate for the knowledge-based foil rejection in the MS model ( 
                            
                               
                                  θ 
                               
                               
                                  k 
                               
                            
                         ) can vary between experimental conditions. Effects that are problematic for SDT theory, such as the effect of target-foil similarity on  
                            
                               
                                  d 
                               
                               
                                  ′ 
                               
                            
                          and the mirror effect, can be readily explained by a differential rate for the  
                            
                               
                                  θ 
                               
                               
                                  k 
                               
                            
                          parameter. 
                            21 
                         
                         
                            21 
                            It is also possible to account for the difference in variance between the target and foil distributions ( Glanzer, Kim, Hilford, & Adams, 1999; Ratcliff et al., 1994, 1992 ) with a MPT model. However, this topic is outside the scope of the present paper. 
                          Consequently the topic of stochastic mixtures for both targets and foils is essential for understanding recognition memory. Yet the detection of a mixture is a challenging problem because mixtures might not be detected even when they in fact exist. The failure to find a mixture does not prove that there is only a single process. A more profitable approach would be to use a MPT model as a tool to see if that model can detect a mixture. For example the  
                            
                               
                                  θ 
                               
                               
                                  k 
                               
                            
                          and  
                            
                               
                                  θ 
                               
                               
                                  S 
                               
                            
                          parameters in the MS model can be estimated, and if these parameters are substantially different than 0, then there is a case for a stochastic mixture. However, if these parameters are trivially small, then a single-process account like a SDT strength model could be a reasonable model.

Although the idea of a mixture on foil trials is the central focus of this paper, MPT models are also capable of detecting the special case of a homogeneous set of foil trials, i.e., the absence of a mixture. In the recent paper by  Chechile et al. (2012) , a new MPT model, called Implicit–Explicit Separation (IES) model, was advanced for obtaining separate estimates for implicit and explicit storage. Like the MS model, the IES model uses a  
                            
                               
                                  θ 
                               
                               
                                  k 
                               
                            
                          parameter to deal with stochastic mixtures on foil test trials.  Chechile et al. (2012)  also describe an experiment where short lists of six memory targets are presented under three different conditions. The conditions differ in the time for target presentation. The presentation times are either 45 ms, 100 ms, or 1000 ms per target. In the first two conditions, the lists are presented so rapidly that memory encoding for the entire list is very poor; however, in the 1000 ms per target condition, the lists are reasonably well encoded. Because the encoding is so poor in the 45 and 100 ms conditions, the  
                            
                               
                                  θ 
                               
                               
                                  k 
                               
                            
                          parameter should be close to zero. If the subjects are unable to encode most of the items on the list, then how can they use list knowledge to reject foils? In fact,  Chechile et al. (2012)  reported that the  
                            
                               
                                  θ 
                               
                               
                                  k 
                               
                            
                          parameter was not reliably different than a trivial value of .01 in those rapid presentation conditions. However, in the condition with 1000 ms per item,  
                            
                               
                                  θ 
                               
                               
                                  k 
                               
                            
                          was .278 and was reliably different from the  
                            
                               
                                  θ 
                               
                               
                                  k 
                               
                            
                          value for the other two conditions. Consequently, MPT models are capable of detecting the absence of a mixture when there is homogeneous processing as well as accurately estimating the mixing rates when there is a mixture.

Finally, the question naturally arises as to why signal detection theory has shortcomings as a model of recognition memory, but is still an excellent model for sensory detection experiments. Although recognition memory tests and sensory detection experiments look quite similar, there are important differences, and these differences can account for the differential effectiveness of SDT. For example, in an audition detection study, the signal is typically a shift on a single sensory quality, e.g., an increase in loudness without changes in pitch, overtones, etc. For such studies, both the signal and noise stimuli have common sensory properties, but differ in the quantitative value on that dimension. Two sensory stimuli presented simultaneously can be ordered on that dimension. From this experimental context, the utilization of signal detection theory is a suitable model for parsing signal sensitivity from response decision criteria. However, memory representations are not likely to be arrayed along a single dimension without extraordinary restrictions on the stimuli. In general, it is not likely that memory encodings are ordered along a single latent axis. Nevertheless, SDT assumes that there is a single dimension on which both the targets and foils are distributed, i.e., the strength axis. It is very likely that the difficulties of the SDT approach for recognition memory have their root in this assumption. A multidimensional signal detection framework, such as the General Recognition Theory ( Ashby & Townsend, 1986 ), provides for a more complex representation of memory items. Nonetheless, applications of multidimensional signal detection have generally been applied to a restricted set of perceptual stimuli. It is difficult to see how arbitrary target items like LRH, which might be encoded by the subject as “Linda Rides Horses”, is represented by a multidimensional space. Yet MPT models, like the MS model, avoid these representation problems by dealing with probability measures for definable categories such as sufficient storage versus insufficient storage. This approach is less ambitious in the memory representation, but it can readily handle mixtures of both targets and foils. The MPT models also have further advantages such as providing a credible accounts of target-foil similarity, and the mirror effect.

The author wishes to thank William Batchelder, Philip L. Smith, and an anonymous reviewer for their valuable comments and criticisms of an earlier draft of this paper.
